<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1638312732479001141">" The Enneagram is an extraordinary framework for understanding more about ourselves. No matter from which point of view we approach it, we discover fresh conjunctions of new and old ideas." So writes Don Riso in this expanded edition of his classic interpretation of the Enneagram, the ancient psychological system used to understand the human personality. In addition to up</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1638312732479001141">" The Enneagram is an extraordinary framework for understanding more about ourselves. No matter from which point of view we approach it, we discover fresh conjunctions of new and old ideas." So writes Don Riso in this expanded edition of his classic interpretation of the Enneagram, the ancient psychological system used to understand the human personality. In addition to updating the descriptions of the nine personality types, Personality Types, Revised greatly expands the accompanying guidelines and, for the first time, uncovers the Core Dynamics, or Levels of Development, within each type. This skeletal system provides far more information about the inner tension and movements of the nine personalities than has previously been published. This increased specificity will allow therapists, social workers, personnel managers, students of the Enneagram, and general readers alike to use it with much greater precision as they unlock the secrets of self-understanding, and thus self-transformation.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1638312732479001141">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer11890950751757780679">I've read (studied) Myers Briggs "please understand me" for over 20 years, so was delighted this book incorporated ties to that.. but took it to another level of personal growth and development... no human fits neatly into 16 boxes like Myers Briggs dictates, something that I have never fully embraced as I tended to conclude folks are compilations of several of the 16 boxes.. 20% of one, 50% of another, etc... basically defining their own identity. However, the principles in this personality boo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11890950751757780679">I've read (studied) Myers Briggs "please understand me" for over 20 years, so was delighted this book incorporated ties to that.. but took it to another level of personal growth and development... no human fits neatly into 16 boxes like Myers Briggs dictates, something that I have never fully embraced as I tended to conclude folks are compilations of several of the 16 boxes.. 20% of one, 50% of another, etc... basically defining their own identity. However, the principles in this personality book are less rigid.. more fluid... you're a core this with a wing that... then there are subset depending on if you are high, middle, or low in your development which changes as we mature and experience different phases in our lives... much better! Enjoyed it. I think I may actually purchase this one for future reference instead of just borrowing it from the library.</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1358288462">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10166615474933084602">I saw a friend mention this book on social media and the idea intrigued me so I checked it out. After reading it I am greatly interested in learning more about the enneagram for it seems incredibly useful. I drove my wife quite nuts talking about the whole thing. I ended up adding 3-4 more books to my amazon wishlist on this topic.<br /><br /><br />At the same time, I honestly did not read every word of the book. I skimmed each chapter to get a quick grasp on the personality type. I eagerly wanted to get to the c</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10166615474933084602">I saw a friend mention this book on social media and the idea intrigued me so I checked it out. After reading it I am greatly interested in learning more about the enneagram for it seems incredibly useful. I drove my wife quite nuts talking about the whole thing. I ended up adding 3-4 more books to my amazon wishlist on this topic.<br /><br /><br />At the same time, I honestly did not read every word of the book. I skimmed each chapter to get a quick grasp on the personality type. I eagerly wanted to get to the chapters I thought I was. After reading, I am still not sure where I fit - 2, 9, or 1. Am I a helper, peacemaker or reformer? I read each of those chapters and see myself in each one in many ways. While I did not read every chapter, I definitely see using this book as a reference in the future.<br /><br />This is the sort of book that people, like me, who work in Christian ministry greatly benefit from reading. We work with people and books like this one are about people, so that is reason enough. I know many churches already use different sorts of personality profiles for staff training and I am seriously going to push for our ministry to use it for both staff and volunteers. It definitely sheds a light on quite a bit about people.</span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5236506472416649129">I would agree. It's funny, because both my wife and I are INFJ pretty consistently, but according to the "exact science" we would not pair well together, but we do. People that take the test too strictly I think are missing the point. Still very illuminating nevertheless.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5236506472416649129">...more</a>

    <br />

    <span class="xhrCommentFooter greyText">
      12 de Ago 16:33

<span id="freeTextContainer18117645529632377851">Right. I think it's great as far as indicating a person's propensities. I just think that all personality tests/profiles are really only static, but p</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18117645529632377851">Right. I think it's great as far as indicating a person's propensities. I just think that all personality tests/profiles are really only static, but personalities are far more dynamic. Traits can change over time and often it depends on the situation whether one will be more inclined to one aptitude rather than another. In my case, I get INTJ and INFJ consistently. Points are usually only within a few of being feeling or thinking. So I'm right on the cusp there. It depends on the situation whether I use more externalized thinking or feeling.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1323119209785734925">I don't like to over-rely on personality tests, but this book gives the singularly best descriptions of people and is more dead-on accurate than any other personality book I've ever read. Even if you are skeptical, this book is extremely interesting. Anyone that's interested in Jungian/Archetypal personality psychology would likely find this book enjoyable. It's great to turn to and look through the pages to place new people you meet.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1288294162">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1288294162" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4643358-dimitris-hall" class="left imgcol" title="Dimitris Hall"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1410023523p2/4643358.jpg" alt="Dimitris Hall" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1288294162?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 25, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4643358-dimitris-hall" class="user" title="Dimitris Hall">Dimitris Hall</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/12173661-personality-types" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4643358-dimitris-hall?shelf=kindle" class="actionLinkLite">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1288294162">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4135352267638016765"><img width="360" height="360" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440568783i/15993892._SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br /><blockquote>Experience has shown that ... personalities ... may be grouped into various major categories, and for purposes of studying them this is a helpful device. Classifications must never be taken too seriously—they ruin much thinking—but the fear to use them has prevented much more thinking.<br /><br />—Karl A. Menninger, The Human Mind</blockquote><br /><br />The above quote would find a lot of people in the world in open disagreement. Even in the US, where different social needs and anxieties gave birth to almost all forms of typology</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4135352267638016765"><img width="360" height="360" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1440568783i/15993892._SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br /><blockquote>Experience has shown that ... personalities ... may be grouped into various major categories, and for purposes of studying them this is a helpful device. Classifications must never be taken too seriously—they ruin much thinking—but the fear to use them has prevented much more thinking.<br /><br />—Karl A. Menninger, The Human Mind</blockquote><br /><br />The above quote would find a lot of people in the world in open disagreement. Even in the US, where different social needs and anxieties gave birth to almost all forms of typology developed today, there is still some skepticism about the extent to which typology works and is based on fact; in the culture I grew up in, namely millennial Greece, the very concept of the existence of a number of more or less concrete personality types, is rather foreign to say the least---ironically, too, because some of the most adamant proto-typologists were ancient Greeks philosophers such as Galen, who is the best-known.<br /><br />My enduring fascination with the subject and my attempts of discussing it with my surroundings have been mostly welcomed with polite indifference and at worst with open contempt: surely the entire wide spectrum of humanity cannot fit in a handful of archetypes. "How is this any different from astrology?", asks a One that has made her mind up about right and wrong; "no system can pigeonhole the infinite complexity that is me" is a common reaction from Threes or special-snowflake disintegrating Fours; "you <em>do</em> know that people's behaviours change according to their surroundings, right?", comes the valid though overly dismissive comment from a Five who likes to think he's unusually smart and thorough.<br /><br />It's been very difficult to get people to look at this seriously and see the strengths of existing typology systems and how they can help us empathise with and understand eachother and ourselves. Half-arsed online tests and the seeming equation of typology with "which Disney/Game of Thrones/famous person are you?" hasn't helped people take the field seriously either, but I'm not one to judge; after all, it is how I myself, and many others I'm sure, originally came across typology. The difference is that I took an interest in the <em>theory</em> of it all, the <em>questions</em> that result in the answers that are all the different types. Thus did my research in this realm begin years ago and ever since I've been slowly trying to follow Kierkegaard's advice to become subjective toward others and objective toward myself.<br /><br />Before reading <em>Personality Types</em>, the typology system I'd been most familiar with was the Myers-Briggs Type Indicator, or MBTI, developed in the '70s and in later years built upon by David Keirsey and his <a rel="nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Keirsey#Development_of_temperament_theories">four temperaments</a>. MBTI was based on Jung's <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.cognitiveprocesses.com/Cognitive-Functions/">eight cognitive functions</a> and laid out the sixteen four-letter type system we know and love today. According to it, each type is a different ordering of these Jungian functions that correspond to each individuals preference of use. I, for instance, <a rel="nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/INFP#Cognitive_functions">am an INFP because I primarily use Introverted Feeling and then Extraverted Intuition</a>.<br /><br />Understanding how the cognitive functions work for each type is essential for understanding the MBTI, a fact which regrettably but understandably is most often missed by online tests because it makes the whole thing about ten times more difficult to decode.<br /><br />To sum up, MBTI is used to categorise people according to their cognitive functions: the mechanics of the manner in which they perceive and process information, how they perceive the world (by observing or by abstracting?) and how they make decisions (thinking their way out or doing what feels right?).<br /><br />Nevertheless, the MBTI isn't even what this book is about; I just wanted to illustrate the difference between it and the Enneagram, which is a different school of typology, and what <em>Personality Types</em> is about. Riso and Hudson did an excellent job with it of presenting the Enneagram as a more organic form of typology than MBTI. Sometimes the latter feels as if it's somehow constructed or artificial; the Enneagram, on the other hand, is very convincingly presented in this book as something that <em>does exist</em> out there, that it is what had been attempted to be captured by the first known typologists in ancient times up to Freud, and consequently <em>it</em> is something that absolutely has to be part of modern psychology and psychotherapy. They make a convincing case that the Enneagram's the culmination of everything that's been done before in the field, the most perfected and complete system that has been developed to this day. And after reading the book, I do stand convinced.<br /><br />Here's a small sample of what the types are about and our problems:<br /><blockquote>
<strong>Twos</strong> spend their whole lives searching for love from others and still feel that they are unloved.<br /><strong>Threes</strong> endlessly pursue achievement and recognition but still feel worthless and empty.<br /><strong>Fours</strong> spend their entire lives trying to discover the meaning of their personal identity and still do not know who they are.<br /><strong>Fives</strong> endlessly accumulate knowledge and skills to build up their confidence but still feel helpless and incapable.<br /><strong>Sixes</strong> toil endlessly to create security for themselves and still feel anxious and fearful about the world.<br /><strong>Sevens</strong> look high and low for happiness [through new experiences] but still feel unhappy and frustrated.<br /><strong>Eights</strong> do everything in their power to protect themselves and their interests but still feel vulnerable and threatened.<br /><strong>Nines</strong> sacrifice a great deal to achieve inner peace and stability but still feel ungrounded and insecure.<br />And finally, <strong>Ones</strong> strive to maintain personal integrity but still feel divided and at war with themselves.<br /><br />The way out of these self-defeating patterns is to see that they cannot bring us the happiness that we seek because our personality does not have the power to create happiness. As wisdom has always recognized, it is only by dying to ourselves—that is, to our ego and its strategies—that we find life.</blockquote><br /><br />Apart from this small sample, here are some of the reasons I think the Enneagram is an excellent tool and theoretical system:<br /><br /><blockquote>• The Enneagram is based on triads, just as the MBTI is based on pairs. Each Enneagram type is the combination of <strong>thinking, feeling or instinct with a modality of overexpression, underexpression or repression</strong>, which in turn represents each type's fundamental characteristic: all at once, its main weakness, the bane of its existence, what it strives to overcome, as well as what it's ambitions are aimed at and what it thinks it lacks. That makes 3 times 3, three modalities for three fundamental aspects of humanity.<br />• The <strong>wing</strong> system adds more depth and intricacy.<br />• On top of that, the fact that if as a person you're expressing your type well you're "integrating" into another type and if you're not you're disintegrating into yet another makes it clear what each type can strive for or can expect to happen if it doesn't remain healthy.<br />• The system is made even more complex by the fact that for each type there are essentially <strong>nine sub-types</strong> according to the level of development of the type. That also goes for the wings and directions of integration/disintegration.<br />• All the above combined make the Enneagram not only a great tool for self-discovery, empathy and understanding, but also quite revealing and useful for self-development as well.<br />• While reading the lengthy descriptions for each of the types, <strong>I had very clear images of real people I know or friends of mine who appear to be embodiments of their types</strong>. Imagine the symbol above but with the faces of people in my social network at each end. My personal Enneagram became these 9 friends of family of mine, and now I believe I can understand their possible fears, troubles and priorities much better, as well as see reflections of those characteristics on myself.</blockquote><br /><br />This stuff is real and I want to get deeper into it. I would heartily recommend you do as well, and there's no better place to start than Reddit's <a rel="nofollow" href="https://www.reddit.com/r/enneagram">Enneagram Subreddit</a> which has all the information and links to tests you might need. When you get the basics, reading an actual book, this one or another good one by Riso and Hudson or other personality psychologists and distinguished writers on the subject, will be the way to go. Good luck!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4135352267638016765">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer31824220801981256">Decent read that goes in-depth into Enneagram types. Could have been even simpler though in terms of identifying the basic core fears of each type; but it has complete write ups of each Enneagram type in various stages of health and disintegration, which is useful. I pegged most of my family and friends quite easily as a result. <br /><br />The Core Fears Simplified:<br />Core fear – agitated, aggression.<br /><br />Core 1: fear of being corrupt. (Strive for perfection.) Agitation: you’re an idiot, pushing you away.<br /><br />Core 2: f</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText31824220801981256">Decent read that goes in-depth into Enneagram types. Could have been even simpler though in terms of identifying the basic core fears of each type; but it has complete write ups of each Enneagram type in various stages of health and disintegration, which is useful. I pegged most of my family and friends quite easily as a result. <br /><br />The Core Fears Simplified:<br />Core fear – agitated, aggression.<br /><br />Core 1: fear of being corrupt. (Strive for perfection.) Agitation: you’re an idiot, pushing you away.<br /><br />Core 2: fear of being unloved. (Smother to compensate.) Agitation: freak out and want to know how you feel about them.<br /><br />Core 3: fear of being worthless. (Become the best.) Agitation: aggressively firing off advice. <br /><br />Core 4: scared of being insignificant. (Become overly expressive.) Agitation: aggressive competitiveness, anger at self for blending in too much. <br /><br />Core 5: fear of inadequacy. (Obsess over obtaining knowledge.) Agitation: disassociate, self-analyze, and shut down.<br /><br />Core 6: fear of being without support and guidance. (Rely too much on advice from others.) Agitation: begging for help, doubting others’ abilities to do things alone.<br /><br />Core 7: fear of not having your desires met. (Scared to commit. Phobic.) Agitation: freak out because making this decision might make them miss other opportunities. <br /><br />Core 8: fear of being harmed or controlled. (Dominate people. Paranoia.) Agitation: become aggressive, obsessed with motivation, and confrontational. <br /><br />Core 9: fear of loss or separation. (Stay in unhealthy relationships.) Agitation: passive-aggressive clinging.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_266069008">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/266069008" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2875480-dawn" class="left imgcol" title="Dawn"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1302211588p2/2875480.jpg" alt="Dawn" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/266069008?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 06, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2875480-dawn" class="user" title="Dawn">Dawn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2875480-dawn?shelf=self-help" class="actionLinkLite">self-help</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2875480-dawn?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2875480-dawn?shelf=library" class="actionLinkLite">library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer266069008">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17367375760905531638">This book has some really useful insights into personality, fears, desires, motivations, and parental relationships. It can be quite complicated and takes a lot of reading to attain the information on your personality type. Also doing the typing is not straight forward. However, the information is worth having and has resulted in some deep thinking for me. It has helped me understand what areas of my personality I need to address in order to improve my life.<br /><br />I didn't like how negative the tone of</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17367375760905531638">This book has some really useful insights into personality, fears, desires, motivations, and parental relationships. It can be quite complicated and takes a lot of reading to attain the information on your personality type. Also doing the typing is not straight forward. However, the information is worth having and has resulted in some deep thinking for me. It has helped me understand what areas of my personality I need to address in order to improve my life.<br /><br />I didn't like how negative the tone of the book is though. The final thoughts on each personality tell of its total demise into suicide, murder, psychopathology, and mental terrors. It is written as if it is human nature to end up this way. The majority of us though will exist in the average levels for the most of our lives. I think more emphasis on how to move out of those levels and into the upper levels would have made the book complete. It does leave you to figure it out for yourself a little too much.<br /><br />The website has lots of useful information and if you wanted just to know about your own personality type, then taking the $10 test would be better than buying the book. Buy the book if you want to learn about all the personalities, not just your own.<br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.enneagraminstitute.com/" target="_blank">http://www.enneagraminstitute.com/</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17367375760905531638">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_161402637">

      <a href="/user/show/8559933-luke" class="user" title="Luke">Luke</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1039582988">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1760918634089113744">I was initially skeptical of the whole enneagram thing, worried it was one of those pop spirituality trends a la The Secret, kinda like Zodiac-Lite meets the Myers Briggs test (instead of 12 signs, there are 9 personality types.) Turns out, it’s not weird or boring at all. Rather, this book gives an insightful and illuminating rundown of the ways different personality types process information and emotions, the various ways we think about ourselves and navigate the world. In the chapter about my</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1760918634089113744">I was initially skeptical of the whole enneagram thing, worried it was one of those pop spirituality trends a la The Secret, kinda like Zodiac-Lite meets the Myers Briggs test (instead of 12 signs, there are 9 personality types.) Turns out, it’s not weird or boring at all. Rather, this book gives an insightful and illuminating rundown of the ways different personality types process information and emotions, the various ways we think about ourselves and navigate the world. In the chapter about my "type," it was freaky to read a (mostly) dead-on description of how I act/think/feel when I'm at my best/worst, and why. I won’t say this book or the enneagram paradigm has necessarily “changed my life,” but it has caused me to think differently about myself and certain people I know (including at least one fictional character I’ve been writing about for a while.) I think I understand a little better now what makes us all tick.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1760918634089113744">...more</a>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer888530319">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12109218259025306655">Although my study of the Enneagram is limited to listening to a set of tapes and reading two books, I would consider this book to be a comprehensive study of each of the nine types. Although a couple of the chapters were more theoretical and read more like a text book and therefore didn’t catch my attention, I found the information for each of the types to be quite helpful in learning to discover oneself and to recognize how one could move towards being healthy by moving in the direction of inte</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12109218259025306655">Although my study of the Enneagram is limited to listening to a set of tapes and reading two books, I would consider this book to be a comprehensive study of each of the nine types. Although a couple of the chapters were more theoretical and read more like a text book and therefore didn’t catch my attention, I found the information for each of the types to be quite helpful in learning to discover oneself and to recognize how one could move towards being healthy by moving in the direction of integration.<br /><br />I particularly appreciated the last chapter, Moving Toward Wisdom, which spoke of things such as faith, seeking meaning in something outside ourselves (as St Augustine said of God, “Thou has made us for Thyself, therefore we may not rest anywhere save in Thee.”), and also moving beyond deception in the direction of self-transcendence, “a rebirth, a true transformation, the coming into being of a new person who is learning to leave the old ways behind and strike out into a new world.” The authors note that the Enneagram is only a tool, something to help us understand ourselves as we really are, at our best and at our worst, and to recognize that “the way out of self-defeating patterns is to see that they cannot bring us the happiness that we seek because our personality does not have the power to create happiness. As wisdom has always recognized, it is only by dying to ourselves – that is, to our ego and its strategies – that we find life.”<br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_172937639">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172937639" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1889852-linz" class="left imgcol" title="Linz"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1388109121p2/1889852.jpg" alt="Linz" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/172937639?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 08, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1889852-linz" class="user" title="Linz">Linz</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer172937639">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13475601065390187623">This is a great, comprehensive book for deep understanding of the Enneagram's 9 core personality types. It offers in depth descriptions of each personality, with approximately 30 pages dedicated to each type, describing 9 different levels of health. Along with those thorough descriptions, there are advanced guidelines, history of the Enneagram, and guidelines on how to use it. Unlike many personality typing systems, the nice thing about the Enneagram is that it doesn't pin a person's behavior do</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13475601065390187623">This is a great, comprehensive book for deep understanding of the Enneagram's 9 core personality types. It offers in depth descriptions of each personality, with approximately 30 pages dedicated to each type, describing 9 different levels of health. Along with those thorough descriptions, there are advanced guidelines, history of the Enneagram, and guidelines on how to use it. Unlike many personality typing systems, the nice thing about the Enneagram is that it doesn't pin a person's behavior down to the personality type, and doesn't DEFINE a person. It is much more fluid, leaving more room for people's uniqueness, while still helping to understand why certain people act the way they do. The nice thing about understanding that is it enables you to see how to improve yourself and understand yourself better, and also helps you to understand others. If you understand another person's personality type, this book will help you understand why they act the way they do and not judge from a biased perspective. <br /><br />The Enneagram Personality System is a typing system that makes complete sense to me. Every personality has a reason to exist, and take one personality system out and everything falls apart. It doesn't just base an entire personality off of four traits that anyone can see from the outside; it digs deep down into the very core of a person to explain the thought process behind certain actions. For this reason, it very nicely explains differences in people's behavior of the same type. People may have the same type and act differently, but deep down, the core thought process behind their actions correlates to their type. A must-read, and it's a great book to have to keep as a reference in case you ever meet someone of a specific type and would like to understand that type better. It's also great for character development. So, yes Enneagram! It has helped me understand myself and people I care about better and is the best personality typing system I know of. :) </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13475601065390187623">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2774360224029447415">A very detailed and comprehensive book on the Enneagram; incredibly helpful for all who are interested in it and/or want to find out their type. Having searched the internet for the past 2-3 years for articles on the Enneagram and hung around PC, INTJf, INFJs, INTP Central, Typology Central and etc to read the discussions on it, I must say that this book really sums up most of the posts I've seen.<br /><br />Apart from the lengthy descriptions of the 9 Types, I particularly liked the section where it linked the Enneagram to Jung's cognitive functions. They have been two of my greatest interests in this area, so I've always been curious to find out how they can be linked (even if it isn't a 100% correlation).<br /><br />Though, the next addition to the book I'm hoping for is the descriptions and theory behind the Tritype; there's a massive lack of articles and posts dedicated to the Tritype on the internet. </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer22134768">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11929088877722604656">If you're ever curious about what motivates people to behave and respond in different manners this system of personality typification is an interesting alternative to the Jung, Freud, and others out there. When you've read enough of this stuff you come to realize that most are the same, they just call it something different.<br /><br />Still, I like Riso's approach for the most part. No book is the be-all, end-all of personality traits but this books is fun and interesting to read if you have an open mind.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11929088877722604656">If you're ever curious about what motivates people to behave and respond in different manners this system of personality typification is an interesting alternative to the Jung, Freud, and others out there. When you've read enough of this stuff you come to realize that most are the same, they just call it something different.<br /><br />Still, I like Riso's approach for the most part. No book is the be-all, end-all of personality traits but this books is fun and interesting to read if you have an open mind. A fun use of this book is to go online with friends and find an Enneagram test; then read up on why Jim Bob always has to go against the crowd (Individualist.) </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer876148254">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3856643230780841031">Great book describing personality types. Based in psychology while recognizing the philosophical, spiritual, and other factors involved in humans. The Enneagram is a complex system, a great next step for those dissatisfied with simplified versions of other personality systems. The authors do a good job explaining what each type is like, how they relate to the world, and how they develop. Generally speaking, the book reads like a text book so don't expect an invigorating read. I skimmed over many</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3856643230780841031">Great book describing personality types. Based in psychology while recognizing the philosophical, spiritual, and other factors involved in humans. The Enneagram is a complex system, a great next step for those dissatisfied with simplified versions of other personality systems. The authors do a good job explaining what each type is like, how they relate to the world, and how they develop. Generally speaking, the book reads like a text book so don't expect an invigorating read. I skimmed over many parts of some chapters. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_933483319">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/933483319" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/31104786-dave-peticolas" class="left imgcol" title="Dave Peticolas"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1399249126p2/31104786.jpg" alt="Dave Peticolas" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/933483319?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 08, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/31104786-dave-peticolas" class="user" title="Dave Peticolas">Dave Peticolas</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer933483319">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6803754165272191309"><p>This is another personality sorter, one more involved than the <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.advisorteam.com/user/ktsintro1.asp">Kiersey</a> sorter. This one has 9 categories, but you can be in two categories at once, and each category has 9 levels of development. I found it difficult to place myself in the categories. There is an on-line <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.enneagraminstitute.com/discover.asp">test</a> available at the <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.enneagraminstitute.com/home.asp">Enneagram Institute</a> but it costs $10.</p></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_398135890">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/398135890" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/8861322-caleb-mason" class="left imgcol" title="Caleb Mason"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1334745705p2/8861322.jpg" alt="Caleb Mason" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/398135890?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 22, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8861322-caleb-mason" class="user" title="Caleb Mason">Caleb Mason</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer398135890">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2825535939909309549">This system dates back to Aristotle. One of the important developments in modern times was breaking each type down into nine levels of healthy to psychotic for each type. And how we are drawn to those types that are bad for us when we need to move toward this who are good. This is not a lightweight pop psych book but is very deeply researched and includes sections analyzing the Eneagram in the contexts of Freud, Jung, Horney and others. This book could change how you see yourself and others. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16096983020151336351">Welllll.......still interested in trying to understand this system of personality types.         <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer86202903">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9528006075080479624">More referenced than read. It explains each of the nine personality types in-depth, and dwells on each type's healthy and unhealthy aspects. The authors made some generalizations that seemingly came from left field, for example, "Individualists (Type 4s) are not close to their parents." What?? A handful of nonsensical observations like that, in addition to the absence of pragmatic pointers for vocations and relationships, made me feel very "eh" about this book.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10893575319074941712">While I agree with your second contention that there are no pragmatic pointers (there are however, in Wisdom of the Enneagram, fyi), I disagree with y</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10893575319074941712">While I agree with your second contention that there are no pragmatic pointers (there are however, in Wisdom of the Enneagram, fyi), I disagree with your comment re: "generalizations." The authors never contend, for instance, that type 4s are not close to their parents. They simply state that Type 4s are not "connected" to either parent. There is a distinct difference between the two and one thing to remember is that the Enneagram works in a spectrum. It is no cut and dry formula.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('P1bah5yRJvUCYQQODjaBjEHkSjcEfXqvEbbN093ynzlaguVUbkMyD465cLVwV2c+CBOJLk2gPQV7v8hBEWdpfA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

For Jung, knowing your type was essential to understanding yourself: a way to measure personal g&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText4795237206313732134\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;The type test inside will tell you about the choices you\'ve made and the direction you\'re taking—according to C. G. Jung\'s theory of psychological types. For Jung, knowing your type was essential to understanding yourself: a way to measure personal growth and change. But his ideas have been applied largely in the areas of career and marital counseling, so type has come to seem predictive: a way to determine your job skills and social abilities. This book reclaims type as a way to talk about people\'s inner potential and the choices they make in order to honor it. Using everyday examples from popular culture—films, &amp;quot;Star Trek,&amp;quot; soap operas, comic strips—it describes the sixteen basic ways people come to terms with their gifts and values. In this book you will find tools to understand:    •  How your personality takes shape    •  How your type reflects not only your current priorities, but your hidden potential    •  How unlived possibilities are trying to get your attention    •  How relationships at home and at work can help you to tap your unrealized gifts Whether you\'re trying to figure out who you are and what you need to do in life, or recognizing that deeper meaning lies beyond what you\'ve already accomplished, this book will help you to become aware of your greatest strengths, your opportunities to live them He called it &amp;quot;the fruit of nearly twenty years\' work in the domain of practical psychology,&amp;quot; and in his autobiography he wrote: &amp;quot;This work sprang originally from my need to define the ways in which my outlook differed from Freud\'s and Adler\'s. In attempting to answer this question, I came across the problem of types; for it is one\'s psychological type which from the outset determines and limits a person\'s judgment. My book, therefore, was an effort to deal with the relationship of the individual to the world, to people and things. It discussed the various aspects of consciousness, the various attitudes the conscious mind might take toward the world, and thus constitutes a psychology of consciousness regarded from what might be called a clinical angle.&amp;quot; In expounding his system of personality types Jung relied not so much on formal case data as on the countless impressions and experiences derived from the treatment of nervous illnesses, from intercourse with people of all social levels, &amp;quot;friend and foe alike,&amp;quot; and from an analysis of his own psychological nature. It speaks from a nonsectarian point of view, unearthing insights from the whole of the Christian tradition, orthodox and heretical, famous and obscure. The esoteric tradition has traditionally searched for meanings that would yield a deeper inner knowledge of the divine. While traditional Christianity draws a timeline from Adam\'s Fall to the Day of Judgment, the esoteric often sees time as folding in on itself, bringing every point to the here and now. While the Church fought bitterly over dogma, the esoteric borrowed freely from other traditions—Kabbalah, astrology, and alchemy—in their search for metaphors of inner truth. Rather than basing his book around exponents of esoteric doctrine, scholar Richard Smoley concentrates on the questions that are of interest to every searching Christian. How can one attain direct spiritual experience? What does &amp;quot;the Fall&amp;quot; really tell us about coming to terms with the world we live in? Can we find salvation in everyday life? How can we ascend, spiritually, through the various levels of existence? What was Christ\'s true message to humankind? From the Gospel of Thomas to A Course in Miracles,  from the Jesus Prayer to alchemy and Tarot, from Origen to Dante to Jung, Richard Smoley sheds the light of an alternative Christianity on these       <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1578956-personality-types-using-the-enneagram-for-self-discovery">Quotes from Personality Types...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“Self-acceptance is a way of viewing oneself compassionately, without condemnation or justification. It is a starting point in life which makes other things possible. It celebrates the fullness of joy of being alive and of being who we are: accepting ourselves, however, does not mean embracing our neuroses or bad habits and celebrating them as if they were virtues. On the contrary, self-acceptance involves loving ourselves enough to accept painful truths about ourselves. . . . Self-acceptance is, at its simplest, the experience of one's self, here and now, as a complete human being, with all the glories and problems that condition entails.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
